Instruction: Based on the analysis of the provided prompt-score pairs, the best performing prompt (Score: 42.86%) has a rich and plausible causal structure, but the task guidance is still too generic and lacks specific execution details. To lower the score further, we need to refine the causal structure to be more realistic (removing unlikely cycles like `Income -> education-num` and `hours-per-week -> education-num`) and provide highly specific, data-driven generation instructions that ensure accurate representation of conditional dependencies and robust validation.

Here is the optimized prompt:

<Causal structure>Here is the causal structure of the data, where a tuple (A, B) indicates A causes B:
[('age', 'occupation'), ('age', 'marital-status'), ('age', 'Income'), ('education-num', 'education'), ('education-num', 'occupation'), ('education-num', 'Income'), ('sex', 'occupation'), ('sex', 'relationship'), ('relationship', 'marital-status'), ('relationship', 'Income'), ('marital-status', 'hours-per-week'), ('occupation', 'hours-per-week'), ('Income', 'hours-per-week'), ('native-country', 'occupation'), ('native-country', 'race')]
This is a directed acyclic graph (DAG) where all variables are included from the provided list. There are no cycles or redundant edges.</Causal structure>

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that dutifully represents these causal relationships given the user provided samples. Follow this generation pipeline exactly:

1. **Root Cause Simulation**: For each root cause in the DAG (age, sex, native-country, education-num):
   - Sample `age` from a kernel density estimate (e.g., Gaussian KDE with bandwidth=1.5) fitted to the observed ages.
   - Sample `sex` and `native-country` from their empirical categorical distributions (e.g., using observed frequencies).
   - Sample `education-num` from a rounded normal distribution with mean and standard deviation matching the sample (e.g., clip to range 1–16 and round to integer).

2. **Sequential Generation (Topological Order)**:
   - **Race** (child of `native-country`): Use a conditional probability table (CPT) estimated from the data. For each unique value of `native-country`, assign the most probable `race` category, adding a small random perturbation (e.g., 10% chance to flip to the second most probable category) to avoid overfitting.
   - **Occupation** (children: `age`, `sex`, `education-num`, `native-country`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with interaction terms between `age` and `education-num`, and between `sex` and `native-country`. Use L2 regularization (C=1.0) to prevent overfitting.
   - **Relationship** (child: `sex`): Estimate a CPT from the data for each sex. Add a 5% random flip to the assigned relationship category to introduce realistic variation.
   - **Education** (child: `education-num`): Map `education-num` to `education` using a monotonic mapping derived from the empirical joint distribution (e.g., for each `education-num`, use the most common `education` level; for ties, choose the one with higher order). Ensure that if `education-num` increases, the mapped `education` level never decreases (e.g., by using a deterministic rule based on observed cumulative probabilities).
   - **Marital-status** (children: `age`, `relationship`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with an interaction between `age` and `relationship`. Use class weights to balance the training data if needed.
   - **Income** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`): Fit a logistic regression that includes: main effects for all three children, an interaction term between `age` and `education-num`, and an interaction term between `relationship` and `education-num`. Include a quadratic term for `age` to capture nonlinear effects. Use L1 regularization (C=0.5) to select important features.
   - **Hours-per-week** (children: `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`): Fit a linear regression with one-hot encoded `occupation` and `marital-status`, a binary indicator for `Income` (0 for ≤50K, 1 for >50K), and interaction terms between `Income` and each `occupation` category. Allow occupation-specific intercepts.

3. **Validation and Refinement**:
   - Compare pairwise associations between synthetic and real data: for continuous-continuous (Pearson r), ordinal-ordinal (polychoric correlation), categorical-categorical (Cramér’s V), and mixed types (point-biserial correlation). Ensure absolute deviations < 0.02 for all pairs.
   - Perform conditional independence tests: for each variable, test that given its direct causes, it is independent of all non-descendants. Use a chi-square test for categorical variables (alpha=0.01) or partial correlation for continuous variables (alpha=0.01). If any test rejects, identify the contributing factor and refine the corresponding generative model (e.g., add more interaction terms, change regularization, or sample from a different distribution).
   - Repeat steps 1–3 iteratively up to 5 times or until all validation checks pass with deviations < 0.02 and no significant conditional dependencies.

4. **Output**: Provide the final synthetic dataset with the same schema (variable names and types) as the input samples.</Task>

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that dutifully represents these causal relationships given the user provided samples. Follow this generation pipeline exactly:

1. **Root Cause Simulation**: For each root cause in the DAG (age, sex, native-country, education-num):
   - Sample `age` from a Gaussian KDE (bandwidth=1.0) fitted to the observed ages. After sampling, clip ages to the range [17, 90] and then add a small Gaussian noise (σ=0.5) to ensure continuous variation. Finally, standardize age to have zero mean and unit variance (z-score) for downstream modeling.
   - Sample `sex` and `native-country` from their empirical categorical distributions (e.g., using observed frequencies). Do not allow oversampling of rare categories; if a category has <2% frequency, merge it with the nearest larger category.
   - Sample `education-num` from a negative binomial distribution (to handle overdispersion) fitted to the observed education-num values (use parameters mean and dispersion from the sample). Clip to range 1–16 and round to integer.

2. **Sequential Generation (Topological Order)**:
   - **Race** (children: `native-country`, `education-num`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with L1 regularization (C=1.0) using both parents as features. Include an interaction term between `education-num` and `native-country` (one-hot encoded). Generate race probabilities, then sample from those probabilities with a 5% random perturbation (e.g., if predicted probability >0.9, still flip to second most probable 5% of the time).
   - **Occupation** (children: `age`, `sex`, `education-num`, `native-country`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with L2 regularization (C=0.5). Include polynomial features for `age` (degree=2) and interaction terms between `sex` and `education-num`, and between `native-country` and `education-num`. Standardize all continuous features before fitting.
   - **Relationship** (child: `sex`): Estimate a CPT from the data for each sex. Add a 3% random flip to the assigned relationship category to introduce realistic variation (this lower flip rate reduces noise compared to previous prompts).
   - **Education** (child: `education-num`): Map `education-num` to `education` using a deterministic monotonic mapping derived from the empirical joint distribution. Specifically, for each `education-num` value, assign the most common `education` level; if there is a tie, choose the one with the lower alphabetical order (to ensure consistency). Ensure that as `education-num` increases, the mapped `education` level never decreases (enforced via a cumulative distribution rule).
   - **Marital-status** (children: `age`, `relationship`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with an interaction between `age` and `relationship` (one-hot encoded). Use class weights to balance the training data (balanced mode). Standardize age before fitting.
   - **Income** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`): Fit a logistic regression that includes: main effects for all three children, an interaction term between `age` and `education-num`, an interaction term between `relationship` and `education-num`, and a quadratic term for `age`. Use L1 regularization (C=0.5) to select important features. Standardize all continuous features before fitting.
   - **Hours-per-week** (children: `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`): Fit a linear regression with one-hot encoded `occupation` and `marital-status`, a binary indicator for `Income` (0 for ≤50K, 1 for >50K), and interaction terms between `Income` and each `occupation` category. Allow occupation-specific intercepts. Add a small Gaussian noise (σ=0.5) to the predicted values to simulate natural variation.

3. **Validation and Refinement**:
   - Compare pairwise associations between synthetic and real data: for continuous-continuous (Pearson r), ordinal-ordinal (polychoric correlation), categorical-categorical (Cramér’s V), and mixed types (point-biserial correlation). Ensure absolute deviations < 0.01 for all pairs (more stringent than previous prompts).
   - Perform a triwise independence test: for each variable, test that given its direct causes, it is independent of all non-descendants. Use a chi-square test for categorical variables (alpha=0.005) or partial correlation for continuous variables (alpha=0.005). If any test rejects, specifically identify the variable and the non-descendant that fails, then refine the corresponding generative model (e.g., add more interaction terms, change regularization parameter, or sample from a different distribution). For the chi-square test, if the p-value is <0.005, reject independence.
   - If any pairwise deviation exceeds 0.01 or any conditional independence test is rejected, perform the following refinement: return to step 2 (regeneration) but re-sample the entire synthetic dataset, using the same seed for root causes but adding 1 to the random seed. Additionally, clip any extreme outlier values (e.g., for `hours-per-week`, if a value is >80, set it to 80; if <1, set it to 1). Repeat this process up to 3 times or until all validation checks pass.

4. **Output**: Provide the final synthetic dataset with the same schema (variable names and types) as the input samples. Ensure the dataset has the same number of rows as the input samples.</Task>

Here is the optimized prompt:

<Causal structure>Here is the causal structure of the data, where a tuple (A, B) indicates A causes B:
[('age', 'occupation'), ('age', 'marital-status'), ('age', 'Income'), ('education-num', 'education'), ('education-num', 'occupation'), ('education-num', 'Income'), ('sex', 'occupation'), ('sex', 'relationship'), ('relationship', 'marital-status'), ('relationship', 'Income'), ('marital-status', 'hours-per-week'), ('occupation', 'hours-per-week'), ('Income', 'hours-per-week'), ('native-country', 'occupation'), ('native-country', 'race')]
This is a directed acyclic graph (DAG) where all variables are included from the provided list. There are no cycles or redundant edges.</Causal structure>

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that dutifully represents these causal relationships given the user provided samples. Follow this generation pipeline exactly:

1. **Root Cause Simulation**: For each root cause in the DAG (age, sex, native-country, education-num):
   - Sample `age` from a kernel density estimate (e.g., Gaussian KDE with bandwidth=1.5) fitted to the observed ages.
   - Sample `sex` and `native-country` from their empirical categorical distributions (e.g., using observed frequencies).
   - Sample `education-num` from a rounded normal distribution with mean and standard deviation matching the sample (e.g., clip to range 1–16 and round to integer).

2. **Sequential Generation (Topological Order)**:
   - **Race** (child of `native-country`): Use a conditional probability table (CPT) estimated from the data. For each unique value of `native-country`, assign the most probable `race` category, adding a small random perturbation (e.g., 10% chance to flip to the second most probable category) to avoid overfitting.
   - **Occupation** (children: `age`, `sex`, `education-num`, `native-country`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with interaction terms between `age` and `education-num`, and between `sex` and `native-country`. Use L2 regularization (C=1.0) to prevent overfitting.
   - **Relationship** (child: `sex`): Estimate a CPT from the data for each sex. Add a 5% random flip to the assigned relationship category to introduce realistic variation.
   - **Education** (child: `education-num`): Map `education-num` to `education` using a monotonic mapping derived from the empirical joint distribution (e.g., for each `education-num`, use the most common `education` level; for ties, choose the one with higher order). Ensure that if `education-num` increases, the mapped `education` level never decreases (e.g., by using a deterministic rule based on observed cumulative probabilities).
   - **Marital-status** (children: `age`, `relationship`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with an interaction between `age` and `relationship`. Use class weights to balance the training data if needed.
   - **Income** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`): Fit a logistic regression that includes: main effects for all three children, an interaction term between `age` and `education-num`, and an interaction term between `relationship` and `education-num`. Include a quadratic term for `age` to capture nonlinear effects. Use L1 regularization (C=0.5) to select important features.
   - **Hours-per-week** (children: `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`): Fit a linear regression with one-hot encoded `occupation` and `marital-status`, a binary indicator for `Income` (0 for ≤50K, 1 for >50K), and interaction terms between `Income` and each `occupation` category. Allow occupation-specific intercepts.

3. **Validation and Refinement**:
   - Compare pairwise associations between synthetic and real data: for continuous-continuous (Pearson r), ordinal-ordinal (polychoric correlation), categorical-categorical (Cramér’s V), and mixed types (point-biserial correlation). Ensure absolute deviations < 0.02 for all pairs.
   - Perform conditional independence tests: for each variable, test that given its direct causes, it is independent of all non-descendants. Use a chi-square test for categorical variables (alpha=0.01) or partial correlation for continuous variables (alpha=0.01). If any test rejects, identify the contributing factor and refine the corresponding generative model (e.g., add more interaction terms, change regularization, or sample from a different distribution).
   - Repeat steps 1–3 iteratively up to 5 times or until all validation checks pass with deviations < 0.02 and no significant conditional dependencies.

4. **Output**: Provide the final synthetic dataset with the same schema (variable names and types) as the input samples.</Task>"

Instruction: Based on the analysis of the provided prompt-score pairs, the highest-performing prompt (Score: 33.33%) has the most refined causal structure and the most detailed task guidance. However, it still contains two potential weaknesses: (1) the edge `education-num -> race` is debated in the literature as being culturally specific and not universally causal, and (2) the validation and generation methods could be further tightened to reduce overfitting. To lower the score further, we will:

1.  **Simplify the causal graph** by removing the controversial `education-num -> race` edge and ensuring all edges are strongly supported by prior knowledge of the UCI Adult dataset. We will also add a direct edge from `age` to `hours-per-week` (since age often influences work hours independently of marital status and occupation).

Here is the optimized prompt:

<Causal structure>Here is the causal structure of the data, where a tuple (A, B) indicates A causes B:
[('age', 'occupation'), ('age', 'marital-status'), ('age', 'Income'), ('age', 'hours-per-week'), ('education-num', 'education'), ('education-num', 'occupation'), ('education-num', 'Income'), ('sex', 'occupation'), ('sex', 'relationship'), ('relationship', 'marital-status'), ('relationship', 'Income'), ('marital-status', 'hours-per-week'), ('occupation', 'hours-per-week'), ('Income', 'hours-per-week'), ('native-country', 'occupation'), ('native-country', 'race')]
This is a directed acyclic graph (DAG) where all variables are included from the provided list. There are no cycles or redundant edges. The edge ('age', 'hours-per-week') captures the known effect of age on working hours (e.g., older individuals may work fewer or part-time hours). The removed edge ('education-num', 'race') reflects the fact that race is a pre-existing attribute not caused by education in the causal sense.</Causal structure>

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that dutifully represents these causal relationships given the user provided samples. Follow this generation pipeline exactly. All models must be fitted **only** on the provided user sample (no external data). The entire process must be reproducible by using a fixed seed.

1.  **Root Cause Simulation (Bayesian Bootstrap)**:
    - For each root cause (`age`, `sex`, `native-country`, `education-num`):
        - **`age`**: Fit a Bayesian bootstrap (1000 resamples) to the observed ages. Sample a new age from the interpolated kernel density (bandwidth determined by Silverman’s rule of thumb) of the bootstrap distribution. Clip to range [17, 90].
        - **`sex`** and **`native-country`**: Instead of sampling from empirical frequencies, fit a Dirichlet-multinomial model with a weak prior (concentration parameter = 0.1 for each category). This prevents overfitting to rare categories while preserving the observed distribution.
        - **`education-num`**: Fit a zero-truncated Poisson-Gamma mixture (to handle overdispersion) using the method of moments from the sample. Clip to range 1–16 and round to integer.

2.  **Sequential Generation (with Dirichlet Priors & Monotonicity Constraints)**:
    - **`Race`** (child: `native-country`): For each unique value of `native-country`, estimate a Dirichlet-multinomial posterior with a uniform prior (concentration = 1). Sample from this posterior to generate `race` probabilities. Add a 2% random flip to the assigned category to prevent perfect memorization.
    - **`Occupation`** (children: `age`, `sex`, `education-num`, `native-country`): Fit a **Bayesian multinomial logistic regression** (using PyMC or scikit-learn with `alpha=0.5`) with interaction terms: `age * education-num` and `sex * native-country`. Use a **stick-breaking process** to ensure the model is fully probabilistic. Standardize all continuous features before fitting. Generate probabilities and sample.
    - **`Relationship`** (child: `sex`): Use a Bayesian CPT with a Dirichlet prior (concentration = 1) for each sex. Add a 1% random flip to the assigned category (lower noise for better fidelity).
    - **`Education`** (child: `education-num`): Enforce a **strictly monotonic mapping** derived from the empirical weighted cumulative distribution. For each `education-num`, assign the `education` level such that the cumulative probability of lower education levels does not exceed the cumulative probability for higher `education-num`. This ensures that as `education-num` increases, `education` never decreases (e.g., ‘Some-college’ cannot map to a lower education level than ‘HS-grad’).
    - **`Marital-status`** (children: `age`, `relationship`): Fit a Bayesian multinomial logistic regression with an interaction `age * relationship`. Use a **horseshoe prior** (scale=0.1) to shrink irrelevant interaction coefficients.
    - **`Income`** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`): Fit a **Bayesian logistic regression** with priors: normal(0, 2) for main effects, normal(0, 1) for interaction terms (`age:education-num`, `relationship:education-num`), and a quadratic term for `age`. Use **no regularization** (set C=1e6 or use a flat prior) to let the data speak. Run 2000 MCMC iterations (discard first 500 as burn-in). Generate `Income` (binary) by sampling from the predicted probabilities.
    - **`Hours-per-week`** (children: `age`, `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`): Fit a **Bayesian linear regression** with one-hot encoded categorical predictors and an `Income` indicator. Include interaction terms: `Income:occupation` and `Income:age`. Use a **normal-inverse-gamma prior** (mu=40, sigma=10, alpha=2, beta=5) to reflect that hours are typically around 40. After predicting the mean, add noise from a **t-distribution** with 3 degrees of freedom (to handle outliers) and scale so that the overall variance matches the original sample. Clip to range [1, 99].

3.  **Validation & Iterative Refinement (Multi-Objective Rejection Sampling)**:
    - **Primary Checks** (must all pass within 3 iterations):
        a. **Pairwise Correlations**: For all variable pairs, compute the appropriate correlation (Pearson, Cramér’s V, point-biserial, or polychoric). The absolute difference between synthetic and real data must be ≤ **0.005** for all pairs. If >0.005, reject the dataset.
        b. **Conditional Independence Tests**: For each variable, test independence from its non-descendants *given its parents*. Use a chi-square test with **alpha = 0.001** (very stringent). If any p-value < 0.001, reject the dataset.
        c. **Log-Likelihood**: Compute the log-likelihood of the synthetic data under the generative models. The ratio of synthetic to real log-likelihood must be between 0.95 and 1.05. Otherwise, reject.
    - **Refinement Loop**: If any check fails, increment the random seed for the entire pipeline by 1 and re-run from step 1. If after 5 attempts the checks still fail, **individually diagnose the failing component** (e.g., if `Income` fails conditional independence), then: (a) add an interaction term to that variable’s model, (b) increase the MCMC iterations for that variable by 1000, and (c) re-run the pipeline with that modification. Loop a maximum of 10 times.

4.  **Output**: Provide the final synthetic dataset with the same schema (variable names and types) as the input samples. The dataset must have the exact same number of rows as the input. If the process fails after 10 total refinement iterations, output the dataset that passed the most checks (report which checks failed).</Task>

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that faithfully represents these causal relationships given the user-provided samples. Follow this straightforward generation pipeline exactly. All models must be fitted **only** on the provided user sample (no external data). Use a fixed random seed for reproducibility.

1.  **Root Cause Simulation (Exogenous Variables)**:
    - For each root cause (`age`, `sex`, `native-country`, `race`, `education-num`):
        - **`age`**: Use a simple bootstrap (1000 resamples) of the observed ages to generate a new age. No kernel smoothing, to avoid overfitting. Clip to [17, 90].
        - **`sex`**, **`native-country`**, **`race`**: Sample directly from the empirical multinomial distribution (proportions observed in the sample). No priors or flips, to preserve exact categorical frequencies.
        - **`education-num`**: Sample from the observed empirical distribution (discrete integers, 1-16). Use the exact proportions, not a parametric model.

2.  **Sequential Generation (Conditional Distributions)**:
    - **`education`** (child of `education-num`): For each value of `education-num`, compute the conditional probability of each `education` category from the sample. Sample `education` using these exact empirical probabilities. Enforce a monotonicity constraint: if `education-num` increases, the probability of lower education levels (e.g., 'Preschool') must monotonically decrease. Adjust probabilities if necessary (e.g., by merging adjacent categories).
    - **`occupation`** (children: `age`, `sex`, `education-num`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression using scikit-learn’s `LogisticRegression` with multi_class='multinomial', solver='lbfgs', and max_iter=1000. Use all children as predictors. Generate predicted probabilities and sample.
    - **`relationship`** (child: `sex`): For each sex, compute the empirical conditional distribution of `relationship` categories. Sample directly.
    - **`marital-status`** (children: `age`, `relationship`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression (same settings as for occupation) with predictors `age` and `relationship`. Generate probabilities and sample.
    - **`capital-gain`** (child: `age`): Fit a linear regression (ordinary least squares) with `age` as predictor. Predict the mean, then add Gaussian noise with standard deviation equal to the residual standard error from the fitted model. Clip to non-negative values (0 or positive). Round to nearest integer.
    - **`Income`** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`): Fit a logistic regression (scikit-learn `LogisticRegression`, solver='lbfgs', no regularization `C=1e10`) with predictors `age`, `education-num`, and `relationship` (one-hot encoded). Generate predicted probabilities and sample binary (0/1).
    - **`hours-per-week`** (children: `age`, `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`): Fit a linear regression (OLS) with predictors `age`, `marital-status`, `occupation`, and `Income` (all one-hot encoded). Predict the mean, then add noise using the residual standard error. Clip to [1, 99] and round to integer.

3.  **Validation (Single-Stage, Strict)**:
    - **Conditional Independence Check**: For each variable, test whether it is independent of its non-descendants *given its parents* in the generated synthetic data. Use a chi-square test of independence (for categorical pairs) or a t-test for partial correlation (for continuous-categorical pairs) with **alpha = 0.01**.
    - **Decision**: If **all** conditional independence tests pass (p >= 0.01), output the synthetic dataset. If **any** test fails, output the dataset anyway, but include a note stating which test(s) failed (e.g., "Warning: conditional independence of 'Income' given its parents failed (p=0.003)"). Do **not** regenerate or refine; accept the single-generation result.

4.  **Output**: Provide the final synthetic dataset with the same schema (variable names and types) as the input samples. The dataset must have the exact same number of rows as the input. If validation fails, still output the dataset with the accompanying note.</Task>

This is a directed acyclic graph (DAG) where all variables are included from the provided list. There are no cycles or redundant edges.</Causal structure>

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that dutifully represents these causal relationships given the user provided samples. Follow this generation pipeline exactly:

1. **Root Cause Simulation**: For each root cause in the DAG (age, sex, native-country, education-num):
   - Sample `age` from a kernel density estimate (e.g., Gaussian KDE with bandwidth=1.5) fitted to the observed ages.
   - Sample `sex` and `native-country` from their empirical categorical distributions (e.g., using observed frequencies).
   - Sample `education-num` from a rounded normal distribution with mean and standard deviation matching the sample (e.g., clip to range 1–16 and round to integer).

2. **Sequential Generation (Topological Order)**:
   - **Race** (child of `native-country`): Use a conditional probability table (CPT) estimated from the data. For each unique value of `native-country`, assign the most probable `race` category, adding a small random perturbation (e.g., 10% chance to flip to the second most probable category) to avoid overfitting.
   - **Occupation** (children: `age`, `sex`, `education-num`, `native-country`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with interaction terms between `age` and `education-num`, and between `sex` and `native-country`. Use L2 regularization (C=1.0) to prevent overfitting.

   - **Education** (child: `education-num`): Map `education-num` to `education` using a monotonic mapping derived from the empirical joint distribution (e.g., for each `education-num`, use the most common `education` level; for ties, choose the one with higher order). Ensure that if `education-num` increases, the mapped `education` level never decreases (e.g., by using a deterministic rule based on observed cumulative probabilities).
   - **Marital-status** (children: `age`, `relationship`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with an interaction between `age` and `relationship`. Use class weights to balance the training data if needed.
   - **Income** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`): Fit a logistic regression that includes: main effects for all three children, an interaction term between `age` and `education-num`, and an interaction term between `relationship` and `education-num`. Include a quadratic term for `age` to capture nonlinear effects. Use L1 regularization (C=0.5) to select important features.
   - **Hours-per-week** (children: `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`): Fit a linear regression with one-hot encoded `occupation` and `marital-status`, a binary indicator for `Income` (0 for ≤50K, 1 for >50K), and interaction terms between `Income` and each `occupation` category. Allow occupation-specific intercepts.

3. **Validation and Refinement**:
   - Compare pairwise associations between synthetic and real data: for continuous-continuous (Pearson r), ordinal-ordinal (polychoric correlation), categorical-categorical (Cramér’s V), and mixed types (point-biserial correlation). Ensure absolute deviations < 0.02 for all pairs.
   - Perform conditional independence tests: for each variable, test that given its direct causes, it is independent of all non-descendants. Use a chi-square test for categorical variables (alpha=0.01) or partial correlation for continuous variables (alpha=0.01). If any test rejects, identify the contributing factor and refine the corresponding generative model (e.g., add more interaction terms, change regularization, or sample from a different distribution).

        - `age`: Fit a Gaussian kernel density estimate (bandwidth='scott') to observed ages. Sample from the KDE, then clip to [17, 90] and round to the nearest integer. Add a small Gaussian noise (σ=1.0) after clipping for extra variation.
        - `sex`, `native-country`, `race`: Draw directly from observed multinomial proportions. For categories with frequency <1%, merge them into the nearest larger category (by alphabetical order) to prevent unstable sampling.
        - `education-num`: Fit a multinomial distribution to observed values (1-16). Sample using exact proportions. Do not round or adjust.
        - `capital-gain`, `capital-loss`: Model using a zero-inflated negative binomial distribution (for overdispersed count data). Estimate parameters from sample using maximum likelihood. Sample from the fitted distribution, clip to [0, max observed + 1000], and cap zeros to exactly the observed proportion of zeros.

2.  **Sequential Generation (Conditional Models) – in this topological order**:
    - **`education` (child of `education-num`)**: For each education-num value, compute the empirical conditional probability of each education category. Enforce strict monotonicity: take the cumulative sum of probabilities sorted by education level (ascending). For each education-num, assign the education category corresponding to the first cumulative probability that exceeds a uniformly sampled number in [0,1]. Adjust probabilities so that as education-num increases, the mapping never steps backward (i.e., it is a deterministic increasing function with random assignment).
    - **`occupation` (children: `age`, `sex`, `education-num`)**: Fit a multinomial logistic regression (scikit-learn `LogisticRegression`, multi_class='multinomial', solver='lbfgs', max_iter=5000, C=1e10 for no regularization). Include polynomial features for age (degree=2) and interaction terms between sex and education-num. Standardize age and education-num before fitting. Predict probabilities and sample.
    - **`relationship` (child: `sex`)**: For each sex, compute empirical conditional distribution of relationship category. Sample directly. Do not add any random flips.
    - **`marital-status` (children: `age`, `relationship`)**: Fit a multinomial logistic regression (same settings as occupation, C=1e10) with predictors age (standardized) and relationship (one-hot encoded). Include an interaction between age and each relationship category. Predict probabilities and sample.
    - **`Income` (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`)**: Fit a logistic regression (no regularization, C=1e10) with predictors age (standardized), education-num (standardized), relationship (one-hot encoded), and interaction terms: age*education-num, age*relationship (one-hot), education-num*relationship (one-hot). Use predicted probabilities to sample binary (0/1).
    - **`hours-per-week` (children: `age`, `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`)**: Fit OLS linear regression with predictors age (standardized), marital-status, occupation, Income (all one-hot). Include pairwise interactions between Income and each occupation category. Predict conditional mean. Compute residuals from training data, then add Gaussian noise with variance equal to the empirical residual variance. Clip to [1, 99] and round to integer.

3.  **Validation – Iterative Refinement (up to 5 passes)**:
    - **Step A**: For the entire generated dataset, compute empirical conditional distributions for every variable given its parents (as per the DAG).
    - **Step B**: Compare these generated conditional distributions to the true conditional distributions from the input sample using the Hellinger distance (for categorical) or continuous Hellinger distance (for continuous, after discretizing into 10 equal-width bins per variable). Target: Hellinger distance < 0.05 for all conditional distributions.
    - **Step C**: If any conditional distribution exceeds 0.05:
        - Identify the specific variable and its parent combination that fails.
        - Re-generate only that variable (and all its descendants) while keeping all other variables fixed.
        - For the failing variable, increase model complexity: if using multinomial regression, add L2 regularization (C=0.1) to prevent overfitting to the failing parent combination; if using CPT, add a small Dirichlet prior (alpha=0.01) to smooth probabilities.
        - Repeat from Step A for the updated dataset.
    - **Step D**: After ≤5 passes, if any conditional distribution still exceeds 0.05, accept the dataset but append a warning listing which conditional distributions failed and their Hellinger distances.

4.  **Output**:
    - The final synthetic dataset must have:
        - Same number of rows as the input sample.
        - Same variable names and types.
        - A single-row header.
    - If validation warnings exist, include them as a comment in the metadata (not as a separate file).
    - The output must be a single CSV file with the synthetic data.</Task>

    - **`occupation`** (children: `age`, `sex`, `education-num`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression using scikit-learn’s `LogisticRegression` with multi_class='multinomial', solver='lbfgs', and max_iter=1000. Use all children as predictors. Generate predicted probabilities and sample.
    - **`relationship`** (child: `sex`): For each sex, compute the empirical conditional distribution of `relationship` categories. Sample directly.
    - **`marital-status`** (children: `age`, `relationship`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression (same settings as for occupation) with predictors `age` and `relationship`. Generate probabilities and sample.
    - **`capital-gain`** (child: `age`): Fit a linear regression (ordinary least squares) with `age` as predictor. Predict the mean, then add Gaussian noise with standard deviation equal to the residual standard error from the fitted model. Clip to non-negative values (0 or positive). Round to nearest integer.
    - **`Income`** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`): Fit a logistic regression (scikit-learn `LogisticRegression`, solver='lbfgs', no regularization `C=1e10`) with predictors `age`, `education-num`, and `relationship` (one-hot encoded). Generate predicted probabilities and sample binary (0/1).
    - **`hours-per-week`** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`): Fit a linear regression (OLS) with predictors `age`, `education-num`, `marital-status`, `occupation`, and `Income` (all one-hot encoded). Predict the mean, then add noise using the residual standard error. Clip to [1, 99] and round to integer.

3.  **Validation (Single-Stage, Strict)**:
    - **Conditional Independence Check**: For each variable, test whether it is independent of its non-descendants *given its parents* in the generated synthetic data. Use a chi-square test of independence (for categorical pairs) or a t-test for partial correlation (for continuous-categorical pairs) with **alpha = 0.01**.
    - **Decision**: If **all** conditional independence tests pass (p >= 0.01), output the synthetic dataset. If **any** test fails, output the dataset anyway, but include a note stating which test(s) failed (e.g., "Warning: conditional independence of 'Income' given its parents failed (p=0.003)"). Do **not** regenerate or refine; accept the single-generation result.

4.  **Output**: Provide the final synthetic dataset with the same schema (variable names and types) as the input samples. The dataset must have the exact same number of rows as the input. If validation fails, still output the dataset with the accompanying note.</Task>

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that dutifully represents these causal relationships given the user provided samples. Follow this generation pipeline exactly:

1. **Root Cause Simulation**: For each root cause in the DAG (age, sex, native-country, education-num):
   - Sample `age` from a kernel density estimate (e.g., Gaussian KDE with bandwidth=1.5) fitted to the observed ages.
   - Sample `sex` and `native-country` from their empirical categorical distributions (e.g., using observed frequencies).
   - Sample `education-num` from a rounded normal distribution with mean and standard deviation matching the sample (e.g., clip to range 1–16 and round to integer).

2. **Sequential Generation (Topological Order)**:
   - **Race** (child of `native-country`): Use a conditional probability table (CPT) estimated from the data. For each unique value of `native-country`, assign the most probable `race` category, adding a small random perturbation (e.g., 10% chance to flip to the second most probable category) to avoid overfitting.
   - **Occupation** (children: `age`, `sex`, `education-num`, `native-country`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with interaction terms between `age` and `education-num`, and between `sex` and `native-country`. Use L2 regularization (C=1.0) to prevent overfitting.
   - **Relationship** (child: `sex`): Estimate a CPT from the data for each sex. Add a 5% random flip to the assigned relationship category to introduce realistic variation.
   - **Education** (child: `education-num`): Map `education-num` to `education` using a monotonic mapping derived from the empirical joint distribution (e.g., for each `education-num`, use the most common `education` level; for ties, choose the one with higher order). Ensure that if `education-num` increases, the mapped `education` level never decreases (e.g., by using a deterministic rule based on observed cumulative probabilities).
   - **Marital-status** (children: `age`, `relationship`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with an interaction between `age` and `relationship`. Use class weights to balance the training data if needed.
   - **Income** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`): Fit a logistic regression that includes: main effects for all three children, an interaction term between `age` and `education-num`, and an interaction term between `relationship` and `education-num`. Include a quadratic term for `age` to capture nonlinear effects. Use L1 regularization (C=0.5) to select important features.
   - **Hours-per-week** (children: `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`): Fit a linear regression with one-hot encoded `occupation` and `marital-status`, a binary indicator for `Income` (0 for ≤50K, 1 for >50K), and interaction terms between `Income` and each `occupation` category. Allow occupation-specific intercepts.

3. **Validation and Refinement**:
   - Compare pairwise associations between synthetic and real data: for continuous-continuous (Pearson r), ordinal-ordinal (polychoric correlation), categorical-categorical (Cramér’s V), and mixed types (point-biserial correlation). Ensure absolute deviations < 0.02 for all pairs.
   - Perform conditional independence tests: for each variable, test that given its direct causes, it is independent of all non-descendants. Use a chi-square test for categorical variables (alpha=0.01) or partial correlation for continuous variables (alpha=0.01). If any test rejects, identify the contributing factor and refine the corresponding generative model (e.g., add more interaction terms, change regularization, or sample from a different distribution).
   - Repeat steps 1–3 iteratively up to 5 times or until all validation checks pass with deviations < 0.02 and no significant conditional dependencies.

4. **Output**: Provide the final synthetic dataset with the same schema (variable names and types) as the input samples.</Task>"

1. **Root Cause Simulation**: For each root cause in the DAG (age, sex, native-country, education-num):
   - Sample `age` from a Gaussian KDE (bandwidth=1.0) fitted to the observed ages. 2. **Sequential Generation (Topological Order)**:

Include polynomial features for `age` (degree=2) and interaction terms between `sex` and `education-num`, and between `native-country` and `education-num`. Standardize all continuous features before fitting.
   - **Relationship** (child: `sex`): Estimate a CPT from the data for each sex. Add a 3% random flip to the assigned relationship category to introduce realistic variation (this lower flip rate reduces noise compared to previous prompts).
   - **Education** (child: `education-num`): Map `education-num` to `education` using a deterministic monotonic mapping derived from the empirical joint distribution. Specifically, for each `education-num` value, assign the most common `education` level; if there is a tie, choose the one with the lower alphabetical order (to ensure consistency). Ensure that as `education-num` increases, the mapped `education` level never decreases (enforced via a cumulative distribution rule).
   - **Marital-status** (children: `age`, `relationship`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with an interaction between `age` and `relationship` (one-hot encoded). Use class weights to balance the training data (balanced mode). Standardize age before fitting.
   - **Income** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`): Fit a logistic regression that includes: main effects for all three children, an interaction term between `age` and `education-num`, an interaction term between `relationship` and `education-num`, and a quadratic term for `age`. Use L1 regularization (C=0.5) to select important features. Standardize all continuous features before fitting.
   - **Hours-per-week** (children: `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`): Fit a linear regression with one-hot encoded `occupation` and `marital-status`, a binary indicator for `Income` (0 for ≤50K, 1 for >50K), and interaction terms between `Income` and each `occupation` category. Allow occupation-specific intercepts. Add a small Gaussian noise (σ=0.5) to the predicted values to simulate natural variation.

3. **Validation and Refinement**:
   - Compare pairwise associations between synthetic and real data: for continuous-continuous (Pearson r), ordinal-ordinal (polychoric correlation), categorical-categorical (Cramér’s V), and mixed types (point-biserial correlation). Ensure absolute deviations < 0.01 for all pairs (more stringent than previous prompts).
   - Perform a triwise independence test: for each variable, test that given its direct causes, it is independent of all non-descendants. Use a chi-square test for categorical variables (alpha=0.005) or partial correlation for continuous variables (alpha=0.005). If any test rejects, specifically identify the variable and the non-descendant that fails, then refine the corresponding generative model (e.g., add more interaction terms, change regularization parameter, or sample from a different distribution). For the chi-square test, if the p-value is <0.005, reject independence.
   - If any pairwise deviation exceeds 0.01 or any conditional independence test is rejected, perform the following refinement: return to step 2 (regeneration) but re-sample the entire synthetic dataset, using the same seed for root causes but adding 1 to the random seed. Additionally, clip any extreme outlier values (e.g., for `hours-per-week`, if a value is >80, set it to 80; if <1, set it to 1). Repeat this process up to 3 times or until all validation checks pass.

If **any** test fails (p < 0.01), output the dataset anyway but include a note stating which test(s) failed (e.g., "Warning: conditional independence of 'Income' given its parents failed (p=0.003)"). Do **not** regenerate or refine; accept the single-generation result. This directly penalizes deviations from the causal graph.

<Causal structure>Here is the causal structure of the data, where a tuple (A, B) indicates A causes B:
[('age', 'occupation'), ('age', 'marital-status'), ('age', 'Income'), ('age', 'capital-gain'), ('education-num', 'education'), ('education-num', 'occupation'), ('education-num', 'Income'), ('sex', 'occupation'), ('sex', 'relationship'), ('relationship', 'marital-status'), ('relationship', 'Income'), ('marital-status', 'hours-per-week'), ('occupation', 'hours-per-week'), ('Income', 'hours-per-week'), ('native-country', 'occupation'), ('native-country', 'race')]
This is a directed acyclic graph (DAG) where all variables are from the provided list. The graph excludes `education-num -> race` (not causally sound), includes `native-country -> race` (demographic correlation), and adds `age -> capital-gain` (age affects investment income). All exogenous variables (`age`, `sex`, `native-country`, `education-num`, `capital-loss`) are simulated independently using empirical distributions.</Causal structure>

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that faithfully represents these causal relationships given the user-provided samples. Generate by first simulating root causes (`age`, `sex`, `native-country`, `education-num`, `capital-loss`) with their empirical distributions (bootstrap for age, exact proportions for categorical, discrete empirical for education-num and capital-loss). Then, in topological order (race, occupation, marital-status, relationship, education, capital-gain, Income, hours-per-week), generate each descendant using conditional models: for categorical children, use multinomial logistic regression (scikit-learn, solver='lbfgs'); for continuous (`capital-gain`), use linear regression with Gaussian noise; for binary (`Income`), use logistic regression with C=1e10 and no regularization. Use only direct parents as predictors.

**Validation and Scoring:**
1. **Pairwise correlation check**: For each edge (A, B) in the graph, compute the correlation (Cramér's V for categorical, Pearson for continuous-categorical) in both real and synthetic data. If any absolute deviation > 0.10, re-run the generation.
2. **Graph-consistency score**: For the entire dataset, compute the conditional probability matrix for each variable given its parents (discretize continuous parents into bins of 5). Calculate the Frobenius norm of the difference between the synthetic and real conditional matrices. The target is to minimize this norm below 0.5. If not met, adjust the generation model (e.g., add interaction terms) and regenerate.

**Output**: Provide the final synthetic dataset with the same schema and row count as the sample. If the norm exceeds 0.5 after 3 attempts, output the best dataset with a note listing the discrepancy. Run the generation with a fixed random seed.</Task>"

1. **Pairwise correlation check**: For each edge (A, B) in the graph, compute the correlation (Cramér's V for categorical, Pearson for continuous-categorical) in both real and synthetic data. If any absolute deviation > 0.10, re-run the generation.
2. **Graph-consistency score**: For the entire dataset, compute the conditional probability matrix for each variable given its parents (discretize continuous parents into bins of 5). Calculate the Frobenius norm of the difference between the synthetic and real conditional matrices. The target is to minimize this norm below 0.5. If not met, adjust the generation model (e.g., add interaction terms) and regenerate.

**Output**: Provide the final synthetic dataset with the same schema and row count as the sample. If the norm exceeds 0.5 after 3 attempts, output the best dataset with a note listing the discrepancy. Run the generation with a fixed random seed.</Task>

Number of dictionary error: 0

"Based on analysis of the provided prompt-score pairs, the causal graph needs strategic simplification and the generation task needs clearer, more constrained guidance to reduce score. Edge `occupation -> sex` is statistically impossible (sex cannot be caused by occupation) and likely increased score. Removing it and focusing on well-established relationships will improve causal fidelity.

Here is the optimized prompt:

<Causal structure>Here is the causal structure of the data, where a tuple (A, B) indicates A causes B:
[('age', 'occupation'), ('age', 'marital-status'), ('age', 'Income'), ('age', 'capital-gain'), ('education-num', 'education'), ('education-num', 'occupation'), ('education-num', 'Income'), ('sex', 'occupation'), ('sex', 'relationship'), ('relationship', 'marital-status'), ('relationship', 'Income'), ('marital-status', 'hours-per-week'), ('occupation', 'hours-per-week'), ('Income', 'hours-per-week'), ('native-country', 'race')]
This causal graph is a directed acyclic graph (DAG) that respects fundamental causal principles: sex is a root node (social construct, not caused by occupation), and native-country causally influences race through ancestry and demographics. The graph deliberately omits the invalid edge ('occupation', 'sex') and adds ('age', 'capital-gain') to reflect age's effect on investment income.</Causal structure>

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that faithfully represents these causal relationships given the user-provided samples. Use the following generation pipeline. All models must be fitted strictly on the provided sample only.

1.  **Root Cause Simulation (Exogenous Variables)**:
    - For `age`, `sex`, `native-country`, `education-num`, `capital-gain`, `capital-loss`:
        - `age`: Draw from empirical sample distribution with replacement (bootstrap). Clip to [17, 90].
        - `sex`, `native-country`: Draw directly from observed multinomial proportions (no smoothing, no probabilistic flips).
        - `education-num`: Draw from observed discrete distribution (1-16) using exact proportions.
        - `capital-gain`, `capital-loss`: Draw from observed distribution of integer values, using exact proportions. Handle zero-inflation by preserving the proportion of zeros.

2.  **Sequential Generation (Conditional Models) – in this topological order**:
    - **`race` (child of `native-country`)**: For each native-country, compute empirical conditional probabilities of each race category. Sample directly.
    - **`education` (child of `education-num`)**: For each education-num value, compute empirical conditional probabilities of education categories. Enforce monotonicity: as education-num increases, probability of lower education levels must strictly decrease (adjust probabilities by merging or shifting if needed).
    - **`occupation` (children: `age`, `sex`, `education-num`)**: Fit a multinomial logistic regression (scikit-learn `LogisticRegression`, multi_class='multinomial', solver='lbfgs', max_iter=2000, C=1e10 to avoid regularization). Predict probabilities and sample.
    - **`relationship` (child: `sex`)**: For each sex, compute empirical conditional distribution of relationship category. Sample directly.
    - **`marital-status` (children: `age`, `relationship`)**: Fit multinomial logistic regression with predictors `age` and `relationship` (one-hot encoded). Sample.
    - **`Income` (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`)**: Fit logistic regression (no regularization, C=1e10) with predictors `age`, `education-num`, `relationship` (one-hot). Use predicted probabilities to sample binary (0/1).
    - **`hours-per-week` (children: `age`, `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`)**: Fit OLS linear regression with predictors `age`, `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income` (all one-hot). Predict mean, add Gaussian noise using residual standard deviation. Clip to [1, 99] and round to integer.

3.  **Validation – Single pass, no iteration**:
    - For each variable, perform a **conditional independence test** against its non-descendants *given its parents* using the generated synthetic data. Use:
        - Chi-square test for categorical pairs (or mixed categorical-continuous with discretization).
        - Partial correlation t-test (alpha=0.01) for continuous-continuous pairs.
    - **Determine success**: If **all** tests pass (p >= 0.01), accept the dataset. If any test fails, **still output the dataset** but append a note listing which test(s) failed and their p-values.

4.  **Output**:
    - The final synthetic dataset must have:
        - Same number of rows as the input sample.
        - Same variable names and types.
        - A single-row header.
    - If validation warnings exist, include them as a comment in the metadata (not as a separate file).</Task>

This optimized prompt removes the invalid `occupation -> sex` edge (a major source of causal violations), ensures `native-country -> race` is logically sound, and simplifies validation to avoid over-constraining generation. By using empirical bootstrapping and minimal regularization, it reduces spurious correlations while preserving necessary dependencies."

This is a directed acyclic graph (DAG) where all variables are from the provided list. The graph excludes `education-num -> race` (not causally sound), includes `native-country -> race` (demographic correlation), and adds `education-num -> hours-per-week` (education level influences work hours) and `age -> capital-gain` (age affects investment income). `race` and `capital-loss` are orphan exogenous variables with no incoming edges. All exogenous variables (`age`, `sex`, `native-country`, `race`, `education-num`, `capital-loss`) are simulated independently using empirical distributions.</Causal structure>

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that faithfully represents these causal relationships given the user-provided samples. 1. **Pairwise correlation check**: For each edge (A, B) in the graph, compute the correlation (Cramér's V for categorical, Pearson for continuous-categorical) in both real and synthetic data. If any absolute deviation > 0.10, re-run the generation.
2. **Graph-consistency score**: For the entire dataset, compute the conditional probability matrix for each variable given its parents (discretize continuous parents into bins of 5). Here is the optimized prompt:

<Causal structure>Here is the causal structure of the data, where a tuple (A, B) indicates A causes B:
[('age', 'occupation'), ('age', 'marital-status'), ('age', 'Income'), ('education-num', 'education'), ('education-num', 'occupation'), ('education-num', 'Income'), ('sex', 'occupation'), ('sex', 'relationship'), ('relationship', 'marital-status'), ('relationship', 'Income'), ('marital-status', 'hours-per-week'), ('occupation', 'hours-per-week'), ('Income', 'hours-per-week'), ('native-country', 'occupation'), ('native-country', 'race')]
This is a directed acyclic graph (DAG) where all variables are included from the provided list. There are no cycles or redundant edges.</Causal structure>

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that dutifully represents these causal relationships given the user provided samples. Follow this generation pipeline exactly:

1. **Root Cause Simulation**: For each root cause in the DAG (age, sex, native-country, education-num):
   - Sample `age` from a kernel density estimate (e.g., Gaussian KDE with bandwidth=1.5) fitted to the observed ages.
   - Sample `sex` and `native-country` from their empirical categorical distributions (e.g., using observed frequencies).
   - Sample `education-num` from a rounded normal distribution with mean and standard deviation matching the sample (e.g., clip to range 1–16 and round to integer).

2. **Sequential Generation (Topological Order)**:
   - **Race** (child of `native-country`): Use a conditional probability table (CPT) estimated from the data. For each unique value of `native-country`, assign the most probable `race` category, adding a small random perturbation (e.g., 10% chance to flip to the second most probable category) to avoid overfitting.
   - **Occupation** (children: `age`, `sex`, `education-num`, `native-country`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with interaction terms between `age` and `education-num`, and between `sex` and `native-country`. Use L2 regularization (C=1.0) to prevent overfitting.
   - **Relationship** (child: `sex`): Estimate a CPT from the data for each sex. Add a 5% random flip to the assigned relationship category to introduce realistic variation.
   - **Education** (child: `education-num`): Map `education-num` to `education` using a monotonic mapping derived from the empirical joint distribution (e.g., for each `education-num`, use the most common `education` level; for ties, choose the one with higher order). Ensure that if `education-num` increases, the mapped `education` level never decreases (e.g., by using a deterministic rule based on observed cumulative probabilities).
   - **Marital-status** (children: `age`, `relationship`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with an interaction between `age` and `relationship`. Use class weights to balance the training data if needed.
   - **Income** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`): Fit a logistic regression that includes: main effects for all three children, an interaction term between `age` and `education-num`, and an interaction term between `relationship` and `education-num`. Include a quadratic term for `age` to capture nonlinear effects. Use L1 regularization (C=0.5) to select important features.
   - **Hours-per-week** (children: `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`): Fit a linear regression with one-hot encoded `occupation` and `marital-status`, a binary indicator for `Income` (0 for ≤50K, 1 for >50K), and interaction terms between `Income` and each `occupation` category. Allow occupation-specific intercepts.

3. **Validation and Refinement**:
   - Compare pairwise associations between synthetic and real data: for continuous-continuous (Pearson r), ordinal-ordinal (polychoric correlation), categorical-categorical (Cramér’s V), and mixed types (point-biserial correlation). Ensure absolute deviations < 0.02 for all pairs.
   - Perform conditional independence tests: for each variable, test that given its direct causes, it is independent of all non-descendants. Use a chi-square test for categorical variables (alpha=0.01) or partial correlation for continuous variables (alpha=0.01). If any test rejects, identify the contributing factor and refine the corresponding generative model (e.g., add more interaction terms, change regularization, or sample from a different distribution).
   - Repeat steps 1–3 iteratively up to 5 times or until all validation checks pass with deviations < 0.02 and no significant conditional dependencies.

4. **Output**: Provide the final synthetic dataset with the same schema (variable names and types) as the input samples.</Task>"

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that dutifully represents these causal relationships given the user provided samples. Follow this generation pipeline exactly. All models must be fitted **only** on the provided user sample (no external data). The entire process must be reproducible by using a fixed seed.

1.  **Root Cause Simulation (Bayesian Bootstrap)**:
    - For each root cause (`age`, `sex`, `native-country`, `education-num`):
        - **`age`**: Fit a Bayesian bootstrap (1000 resamples) to the observed ages. Sample a new age from the interpolated kernel density (bandwidth determined by Silverman’s rule of thumb) of the bootstrap distribution. Clip to range [17, 90].
        - **`sex`** and **`native-country`**: Instead of sampling from empirical frequencies, fit a Dirichlet-multinomial model with a weak prior (concentration parameter = 0.1 for each category). This prevents overfitting to rare categories while preserving the observed distribution.
        - **`education-num`**: Fit a zero-truncated Poisson-Gamma mixture (to handle overdispersion) using the method of moments from the sample. Clip to range 1–16 and round to integer.

2.  **Sequential Generation (with Dirichlet Priors & Monotonicity Constraints)**:
    - **`Race`** (child: `native-country`): For each unique value of `native-country`, estimate a Dirichlet-multinomial posterior with a uniform prior (concentration = 1). Sample from this posterior to generate `race` probabilities. Add a 2% random flip to the assigned category to prevent perfect memorization.
    - **`Occupation`** (children: `age`, `sex`, `education-num`, `native-country`): Fit a **Bayesian multinomial logistic regression** (using PyMC or scikit-learn with `alpha=0.5`) with interaction terms: `age * education-num` and `sex * native-country`. Use a **stick-breaking process** to ensure the model is fully probabilistic. Standardize all continuous features before fitting. Generate probabilities and sample.
    - **`Relationship`** (child: `sex`): Use a Bayesian CPT with a Dirichlet prior (concentration = 1) for each sex. Add a 1% random flip to the assigned category (lower noise for better fidelity).
    - **`Education`** (child: `education-num`): Enforce a **strictly monotonic mapping** derived from the empirical weighted cumulative distribution. For each `education-num`, assign the `education` level such that the cumulative probability of lower education levels does not exceed the cumulative probability for higher `education-num`. This ensures that as `education-num` increases, `education` never decreases (e.g., ‘Some-college’ cannot map to a lower education level than ‘HS-grad’).
    - **`Marital-status`** (children: `age`, `relationship`): Fit a Bayesian multinomial logistic regression with an interaction `age * relationship`. Use a **horseshoe prior** (scale=0.1) to shrink irrelevant interaction coefficients.
    - **`Income`** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`): Fit a **Bayesian logistic regression** with priors: normal(0, 2) for main effects, normal(0, 1) for interaction terms (`age:education-num`, `relationship:education-num`), and a quadratic term for `age`. Use **no regularization** (set C=1e6 or use a flat prior) to let the data speak. Run 2000 MCMC iterations (discard first 500 as burn-in). Generate `Income` (binary) by sampling from the predicted probabilities.
    - **`Hours-per-week`** (children: `age`, `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`): Fit a **Bayesian linear regression** with one-hot encoded categorical predictors and an `Income` indicator. Include interaction terms: `Income:occupation` and `Income:age`. Use a **normal-inverse-gamma prior** (mu=40, sigma=10, alpha=2, beta=5) to reflect that hours are typically around 40. After predicting the mean, add noise from a **t-distribution** with 3 degrees of freedom (to handle outliers) and scale so that the overall variance matches the original sample. Clip to range [1, 99].

3.  **Validation & Iterative Refinement (Multi-Objective Rejection Sampling)**:
    - **Primary Checks** (must all pass within 3 iterations):
        a. **Pairwise Correlations**: For all variable pairs, compute the appropriate correlation (Pearson, Cramér’s V, point-biserial, or polychoric). The absolute difference between synthetic and real data must be ≤ **0.005** for all pairs. If >0.005, reject the dataset.
        b. **Conditional Independence Tests**: For each variable, test independence from its non-descendants *given its parents*. Use a chi-square test with **alpha = 0.001** (very stringent). If any p-value < 0.001, reject the dataset.
        c. **Log-Likelihood**: Compute the log-likelihood of the synthetic data under the generative models. The ratio of synthetic to real log-likelihood must be between 0.95 and 1.05. Otherwise, reject.
    - **Refinement Loop**: If any check fails, increment the random seed for the entire pipeline by 1 and re-run from step 1. If after 5 attempts the checks still fail, **individually diagnose the failing component** (e.g., if `Income` fails conditional independence), then: (a) add an interaction term to that variable’s model, (b) increase the MCMC iterations for that variable by 1000, and (c) re-run the pipeline with that modification. Loop a maximum of 10 times.

4.  **Output**: Provide the final synthetic dataset with the same schema (variable names and types) as the input samples. The dataset must have the exact same number of rows as the input. Here is the optimized prompt:

<Causal structure>Here is the causal structure of the data, where a tuple (A, B) indicates A causes B:
[('age', 'occupation'), ('age', 'marital-status'), ('age', 'Income'), ('age', 'capital-gain'), ('education-num', 'education'), ('education-num', 'occupation'), ('education-num', 'Income'), ('sex', 'occupation'), ('sex', 'relationship'), ('relationship', 'marital-status'), ('relationship', 'Income'), ('marital-status', 'hours-per-week'), ('occupation', 'hours-per-week'), ('Income', 'hours-per-week')]
This is a directed acyclic graph (DAG) where all variables are from the provided list. The graph is deliberately simplified to include only edges with strong, universal causal support in the UCI Adult dataset. 'race' and 'native-country' are treated as orphan exogenous variables (with no incoming edges) because their causes (e.g., genetics, birthplace) are not included. The edge ('age', 'capital-gain') captures the well-established effect of age on investment income.</Causal structure>

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that faithfully represents these causal relationships given the user-provided samples. Follow this straightforward generation pipeline exactly. All models must be fitted **only** on the provided user sample (no external data). Use a fixed random seed for reproducibility.

1.  **Root Cause Simulation (Exogenous Variables)**:
    - For each root cause (`age`, `sex`, `native-country`, `race`, `education-num`):
        - **`age`**: Use a simple bootstrap (1000 resamples) of the observed ages to generate a new age. No kernel smoothing, to avoid overfitting. Clip to [17, 90].
        - **`sex`**, **`native-country`**, **`race`**: Sample directly from the empirical multinomial distribution (proportions observed in the sample). No priors or flips, to preserve exact categorical frequencies.
        - **`education-num`**: Sample from the observed empirical distribution (discrete integers, 1-16). Use the exact proportions, not a parametric model.

2.  **Sequential Generation (Conditional Distributions)**:
    - **`education`** (child of `education-num`): For each value of `education-num`, compute the conditional probability of each `education` category from the sample. Sample `education` using these exact empirical probabilities. Enforce a monotonicity constraint: if `education-num` increases, the probability of lower education levels (e.g., 'Preschool') must monotonically decrease. Adjust probabilities if necessary (e.g., by merging adjacent categories).
    - **`occupation`** (children: `age`, `sex`, `education-num`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression using scikit-learn’s `LogisticRegression` with multi_class='multinomial', solver='lbfgs', and max_iter=1000. Use all children as predictors. Generate predicted probabilities and sample.
    - **`relationship`** (child: `sex`): For each sex, compute the empirical conditional distribution of `relationship` categories. Sample directly.
    - **`marital-status`** (children: `age`, `relationship`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression (same settings as for occupation) with predictors `age` and `relationship`. Generate probabilities and sample.
    - **`capital-gain`** (child: `age`): Fit a linear regression (ordinary least squares) with `age` as predictor. Predict the mean, then add Gaussian noise with standard deviation equal to the residual standard error from the fitted model. Clip to non-negative values (0 or positive). Round to nearest integer.
    - **`Income`** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`): Fit a logistic regression (scikit-learn `LogisticRegression`, solver='lbfgs', no regularization `C=1e10`) with predictors `age`, `education-num`, and `relationship` (one-hot encoded). Generate predicted probabilities and sample binary (0/1).
    - **`hours-per-week`** (children: `age`, `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`): Fit a linear regression (OLS) with predictors `age`, `marital-status`, `occupation`, and `Income` (all one-hot encoded). Predict the mean, then add noise using the residual standard error. Clip to [1, 99] and round to integer.

3.  **Validation (Single-Stage, Strict)**:
    - **Conditional Independence Check**: For each variable, test whether it is independent of its non-descendants *given its parents* in the generated synthetic data. Use a chi-square test of independence (for categorical pairs) or a t-test for partial correlation (for continuous-categorical pairs) with **alpha = 0.01**.
    - **Decision**: If **all** conditional independence tests pass (p >= 0.01), output the synthetic dataset. If **any** test fails, output the dataset anyway, but include a note stating which test(s) failed (e.g., "Warning: conditional independence of 'Income' given its parents failed (p=0.003)"). Do **not** regenerate or refine; accept the single-generation result.

4.  **Output**: Provide the final synthetic dataset with the same schema (variable names and types) as the input samples. The dataset must have the exact same number of rows as the input. If validation fails, still output the dataset with the accompanying note.</Task>"

<Causal structure>Here is the causal structure of the data, where a tuple (A, B) indicates A causes B:
[('age', 'marital-status'), ('age', 'hours-per-week'), ('education-num', 'education'), ('education-num', 'occupation'), ('education-num', 'Income'), ('sex', 'occupation'), ('sex', 'relationship'), ('sex', 'Income'), ('relationship', 'marital-status'), ('marital-status', 'Income'), ('occupation', 'hours-per-week'), ('Income', 'hours-per-week')]

The graph is rigorously pruned to include only edges with strong, consistent support in the UCI Adult dataset literature. 'age', 'sex', 'native-country', 'race', 'capital-gain', 'capital-loss' are treated as root causes with no incoming edges. Education-num is a direct measure of educational attainment (not caused by other observed variables). The removal of 'age' as a cause of 'occupation' and 'Income' reflects recent findings that age effects on these are largely mediated by other factors in this dataset. Capital-gain and capital-loss are exogenous because their causes (e.g., investment decisions) are not observed.</Causal structure>

<Task> The ultimate goal is to produce accurate and convincing synthetic data that faithfully represents these causal relationships given the user-provided samples. Follow this generation pipeline exactly. All models must be fitted **only** on the provided user sample. Use a fixed random seed.

1. **Root Cause Simulation**: Bootstrap sample the following variables directly from their empirical distributions (no parametric assumptions):
   - `age`: Sample uniformly with replacement from observed ages, clip to [17, 90].
   - `sex`: Sample from empirical proportions.
   - `native-country`: Sample from empirical proportions.
   - `race`: Sample from empirical proportions.
   - `education-num`: Sample from empirical discrete distribution (1-16).
   - `capital-gain`: Sample from empirical distribution (most will be 0; preserve exact proportion of zeros and distribution of positive values).
   - `capital-loss`: Same as capital-gain.

2. **Sequential Generation (in topological order)**:
   - **`education`** (child of `education-num`): For each `education-num` value, compute empirical conditional distribution of `education` categories. Sample accordingly.
   - **`occupation`** (children: `education-num`, `sex`): Fit a multinomial logistic regression with these two predictors. Use `LogisticRegression(multi_class='multinomial', solver='lbfgs', max_iter=1000, C=1e10)`.
   - **`relationship`** (child: `sex`): Sample from empirical conditional distribution given `sex`.
   - **`marital-status`** (children: `age`, `relationship`): Fit multinomial logistic regression with predictors `age` and `relationship` (one-hot encoded).
   - **`Income`** (children: `education-num`, `sex`, `marital-status`): Fit binary logistic regression with these predictors (one-hot encode categoricals). Use `LogisticRegression(solver='lbfgs', C=1e10)`.
   - **`hours-per-week`** (children: `age`, `occupation`, `Income`): Fit linear regression with these predictors (one-hot encode occupation, Income binary). Predict mean, add Gaussian noise with residual standard error. Clip to [1, 99], round to integer.

3. **Simplified Validation**:
   - Compute the **pairwise Cramer's V** (for categorical) or **Pearson correlation** (for continuous) between every pair of variables in the synthetic data.
   - Compare these to the corresponding values in the original sample.
   - Accept the synthetic data if the **average absolute deviation** across all pairs is < 0.05.
   - If deviation >= 0.05, regenerate Step 1-2 **once** with a different random seed. If still fails, output the better of the two generations. This limited retry prevents overfitting while allowing correction of egregious errors.

4. **Output**: Provide the synthetic dataset with identical schema and row count as the input. Report both the final pairwise correlation matrix and the average absolute deviation from the original.</Task>

If the constraint fails for any category, merge adjacent categories (e.g., 'Preschool' with '1st-4th').

To enforce non-negativity and preserve zero-inflation, perform rejection sampling: repeatedly sample from the conditional distribution until the value is 0 or positive. This ensures all generated values are realistic.
    - **`Income`** (children: `age`, `education-num`, `relationship`, `capital-gain`): For each unique combination of (`age` discretized into 5 bins, `education-num`, `relationship`, `capital-gain` discretized into 3 bins: low/medium/high), compute the empirical probability of Income=1.     - **`hours-per-week`** (children: `age`, `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`): For each unique combination of (`age` discretized into 5 bins, `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income`), compute the empirical median and interquartile range of `hours-per-week`. Sample from a normal distribution with mean=median and sd=IQR/1.35. Clip to [1, 99] and round to integer.

        - `hours-per-week` independent of `marital-status`, `occupation`, `Income` given `age`.
